Draft the week's newsletter (`playbooks/newsletter.md`) + push copy\n    42→   (`playbooks/push.md`) into `engine/drafts/`; send from MailerLite/OneSignal.\n    43→7. Commit & push.\n    44→\n    45→## Retention loops (free infra already in place)\n    46→\n    47→- **Rich results**: keep every calculator's FAQ + HowTo schema filled → higher SERP CTR.\n    48→- **Newsletter**: MailerLite (`NewsletterSignup.astro`). Optionally enable MailerLite\n    49→  RSS automation off `/rss.xml` so new content auto-emails with no manual step.\n    50→- **Push**: OneSignal (`PushNotification.astro`) — fire on each new-calculator batch.\n    51→\n    52→## KPIs to watch (GA4 `G-HD4P633GWP`)\n    53→\n    54→Organic landing pages & impressions, returning-user %, pages/session, newsletter\n    55→signups, push opt-ins. -1 : 1;\n    return { num: sign * (Math.abs(whole) * den + Math.abs(num)), den: den };\n  }\n\n  function persistUrl(state) {\n    var params = new URLSearchParams();\n    for (var k in state) params.set(k, state[k]);\n    history.replaceState(null, '', '?' + params.toString());\n  }\n\n  function loadUrl() {\n    var params = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);\n    var fields = ['whole1','num1','den1','whole2','num2','den2','op'];\n    fields.forEach(function (id) {\n      var val = params.get(id);\n      if (val === null) return;\n      if (id === 'op') {\n        document.querySelectorAll('.op-btn').forEach(function (btn) {\n          btn.classList.toggle('active', btn.dataset.op === val);\n        });\n      } else {\n        var el = document.getElementById(id);\n        if (el) el.value = val;\n      }\n    });\n  }\n\n  function getOp() {\n    var active = document.querySelector('.op-btn.active');\n    return active ? active.dataset.op : 'add';\n  }\n\n  function opSymbol(op) {\n    return { add: '+', subtract: '−', multiply: '×', divide: '÷' }[op] || '+';\n  }\n\n  function compute(f1, f2, op) {\n    var rn, rd;\n    if (op === 'add') {\n      rn = f1.num * f2.den + f2.num * f1.den;\n      rd = f1.den * f2.den;\n    } else if (op === 'subtract') {\n      rn = f1.num * f2.den - f2.num * f1.den;\n      rd = f1.den * f2.den;\n    } else if (op === 'multiply') {\n      rn = f1.num * f2.num;\n      rd = f1.den * f2.den;\n    } else {\n      if (f2.num === 0) return null;\n      rn = f1.num * f2.den;\n      rd = f1.den * f2.num;\n    }\n    return simplify(rn, rd);\n  }\n\n  function buildSteps(f1raw, f2raw, f1, f2, op, raw, result) {\n    var sym = opSymbol(op);\n    var lines = [];\n    if (f1raw.whole !== 0 || f2raw.whole !== 0) {\n      lines.push('<li>Convert mixed numbers to improper fractions: <strong>' +\n        (f1raw.whole !== 0 ? {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01UgPkwKknEguAAnZGS5LvqW","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→## Intro\n     2→\n     3→TODO: hook the reader and state what they'll learn.\n     4→\n     5→## TODO Section\n     6→\n     7→TODO body. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01D1Aaj46F7grtEzDeWdamiR","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01WSdbpRmSAmNXHEuptG8ZW5","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/home/runner/work/calccrunch/calccrunch/src/content/blog/fraction-calculator-guide.md","content":"Fractions appear constantly in everyday life — cooking, carpentry, splitting a bill, or converting measurements — yet many people freeze when they have to add 3/4 and 2/3 in their head. The good news is that each of the four fraction operations follows a simple, repeatable rule. Learn the rule once, and any fraction problem becomes mechanical.\n\nThis guide walks through **addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division of fractions** with worked examples for each, plus a section on mixed numbers. Skip to the operation you need, or use the [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) to check your work instantly.\n\n## The Four Rules at a Glance\n\n| Operation | Rule | Example |\n|---|---|---|\n| Addition (a/b + c/d) | Find a common denominator, add numerators | 1/2 + 1/3 = 3/6 + 2/6 = **5/6** |\n| Subtraction (a/b − c/d) | Find a common denominator, subtract numerators | 3/4 − 1/6 = 9/12 − 2/12 = **7/12** |\n| Multiplication (a/b × c/d) | Multiply across: (a×c)/(b×d) | 2/3 × 3/4 = 6/12 = **1/2** |\n| Division (a/b ÷ c/d) | Multiply by reciprocal: (a/b) × (d/c) | 2/3 ÷ 4/5 = 2/3 × 5/4 = **5/6** |\n\n## How to Add Fractions\n\nAdding fractions requires a **common denominator** — both fractions must share the same bottom number before you can add the tops.\n\n**Step 1: Check the denominators.** If they're the same, skip to step 3. If not, find a common denominator.\n\n**Step 2: Find the common denominator.** The easiest method: multiply the two denominators together. You can also find the least common multiple (LCM) for cleaner numbers, but multiplying always works.\n\n**Step 3: Convert each fraction.** Multiply the numerator and denominator of each fraction so both denominators become your common denominator.\n\n**Step 4: Add the numerators.** Keep the common denominator.\n\n**Step 5: Simplify.** Divide numerator and denominator by their Greatest Common Factor (GCF).\n\n### Example: 1/2 + 1/3\n\n1. Different denominators: 2 and 3\n2. Common denominator: 2 × 3 = **6**\n3. Convert: 1/2 → 3/6 and 1/3 → 2/6\n4. Add: 3/6 + 2/6 = **5/6**\n5. Already simplified (GCF of 5 and 6 is 1)\n\n### Example: 3/8 + 1/4\n\n1. Common denominator: 8 (since 4 goes into 8 evenly)\n2. Convert: 1/4 → 2/8\n3. Add: 3/8 + 2/8 = **5/8** ✓\n\n<div class=\"callout\">\n<strong>Same-denominator shortcut:</strong> When denominators already match, just add the numerators. 3/7 + 2/7 = 5/7. Done.\n</div>\n\n## How to Subtract Fractions\n\nSubtraction follows the exact same steps as addition — the only difference is that you subtract the numerators in step 4.\n\n### Example: 5/6 − 1/4\n\n1. Different denominators: 6 and 4\n2. Common denominator: 6 × 4 = 24 (or LCM = 12, which keeps numbers smaller)\n3. Using 12: 5/6 → 10/12, 1/4 → 3/12\n4. Subtract: 10/12 − 3/12 = **7/12**\n5. GCF(7, 12) = 1, already simplified ✓\n\n### Example: 7/10 − 3/10\n\nSame denominator: 7/10 − 3/10 = **4/10 = 2/5** (simplified by dividing by 2)\n\nUse the [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) to verify any subtraction and see every step shown automatically.\n\n## How to Multiply Fractions\n\nMultiplication is the easiest operation: **no common denominator needed**. Multiply straight across.\n\n**Rule:** (a/b) × (c/d) = (a×c) / (b×d), then simplify.\n\n### Example: 2/3 × 3/4\n\n- Multiply numerators: 2 × 3 = 6\n- Multiply denominators: 3 × 4 = 12\n- Result: 6/12 = **1/2** (simplified by GCF = 6)\n\n### Example: 5/6 × 4/9\n\n- 5 × 4 = 20, 6 × 9 = 54\n- 20/54 → GCF(20, 54) = 2 → **10/27**\n\n**Cross-cancellation tip:** Before multiplying, check if any numerator and diagonal denominator share a common factor. Cancel them first to keep numbers small. For 2/3 × 3/4: the 3 in the numerator of the second fraction and the 3 in the denominator of the first cancel to 1, giving 2/1 × 1/4 = 2/4 = 1/2. Same answer, smaller arithmetic.\n\n## How to Divide Fractions\n\nDivision uses the \"keep, change, flip\" method:\n\n1. **Keep** the first fraction as-is\n2. **Change** ÷ to ×\n3. **Flip** the second fraction (take its reciprocal)\n4. Multiply normally and simplify\n\n### Example: 3/4 ÷ 1/2\n\n- Keep 3/4, change to ×, flip 1/2 to 2/1\n- 3/4 × 2/1 = 6/4 = **3/2 = 1 1/2**\n\n### Example: 5/8 ÷ 5/4\n\n- Flip 5/4 → 4/5\n- 5/8 × 4/5 = 20/40 = **1/2** ✓\n\n<div class=\"callout\">\n<strong>Why does \"flip and multiply\" work?</strong> Dividing by a number is the same as multiplying by its reciprocal. 6 ÷ 2 = 6 × (1/2) = 3. The same logic extends to fractions.\n</div>\n\n## Working with Mixed Numbers\n\nA mixed number combines a whole number and a fraction (like 2 3/4). Before you can do arithmetic, convert it to an improper fraction:\n\n**Formula:** Whole × Denominator + Numerator, over the original denominator\n\n| Mixed Number | Conversion | Improper Fraction |\n|---|---|---|\n| 1 1/2 | (1×2)+1 / 2 | 3/2 |\n| 2 3/4 | (2×4)+3 / 4 | 11/4 |\n| 3 2/3 | (3×3)+2 / 3 | 11/3 |\n| 4 1/8 | (4×8)+1 / 8 | 33/8 |\n\n### Example: 1 1/2 + 2/3\n\n1. Convert 1 1/2 → 3/2\n2. Add: 3/2 + 2/3 — find common denominator 6\n3. 9/6 + 4/6 = 13/6\n4. Convert back to mixed number: 13 ÷ 6 = 2 with remainder 1, so **2 1/6**\n\n### Example: 2 3/4 × 1 1/3\n\n1. Convert: 2 3/4 → 11/4 and 1 1/3 → 4/3\n2. Multiply: 11/4 × 4/3 = 44/12 = **11/3 = 3 2/3**\n\nThe [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) handles mixed number input directly — type the whole number into the \"Whole Number\" field and let the calculator do the conversion.\n\n## Simplifying Fractions (Reducing to Lowest Terms)\n\nA fraction is **fully simplified** when the numerator and denominator share no common factor other than 1.\n\n**Method:** Find the GCF of the numerator and denominator, then divide both by it.\n\n| Fraction | GCF | Simplified |\n|---|---|---|\n| 6/12 | 6 | 1/2 |\n| 15/25 | 5 | 3/5 |\n| 18/24 | 6 | 3/4 |\n| 14/21 | 7 | 2/3 |\n\n**Finding the GCF:** List the factors of each number and find the largest one they share, or use the Euclidean algorithm (divide the larger by the smaller, take the remainder, repeat until remainder is 0).\n\nFractions also relate directly to percentages — for quick conversions between the two, the [percentage calculator](/calculators/percentage-calculator) is useful. When working out discounts (e.g., a 1/4-off sale = 25% off), the [discount calculator](/calculators/discount-calculator) gives you the final price instantly.\n\n## Frequently Asked Questions\n\n**What is a common denominator and why do I need one to add or subtract fractions?**\nA common denominator is a shared bottom number that lets you compare or combine fractions. You can only add or subtract fractions when they represent equal-sized parts. 1/4 and 1/3 are different-sized pieces, so you can't directly add them — you first convert both to twelfths (3/12 and 4/12) and then add to get 7/12.\n\n**Can the denominator be negative?**\nBy convention, fractions are written with a positive denominator. If you end up with a negative denominator, multiply both numerator and denominator by −1. For example, 3/(−4) = (−3)/4.\n\n**What is an improper fraction?**\nAn improper fraction has a numerator larger than (or equal to) its denominator, such as 7/4. It represents a value greater than 1. It can also be written as the mixed number 1 3/4. Both forms are equally correct; improper fractions are easier to multiply and divide.\n\n**How do I add three or more fractions?**\nAdd two fractions at a time. Find a common denominator for the first two, add them, then add the result to the third fraction. Or find a common denominator for all fractions at once (the LCM of all denominators) and convert each.\n\n**Do I always need to simplify my answer?**\nIn most school and everyday contexts, yes — a simplified fraction is the expected final form. The [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) automatically simplifies every result for you.\n\n## The Bottom Line\n\nEvery fraction operation follows a simple pattern:\n\n- **Add/Subtract:** common denominator first, then combine numerators\n- **Multiply:** straight across, then simplify\n- **Divide:** flip the second fraction, then multiply\n\nFor mixed numbers, convert to improper fractions before operating. And whenever you need to check your work or handle complex calculations, the [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) shows the full step-by-step solution so you can see exactly where each number comes from.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"stop_details":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":297,"cache_read_input_tokens":74353,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":297,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":72,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"}},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"0aba2aef-5370-4f6d-992f-47ee58dd3d79","uuid":"c1b0931d-6940-4d35-8016-a615e494ce60"}

Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:\n     1→Fractions appear constantly in everyday life — cooking, carpentry, splitting a bill, or converting measurements — yet many people freeze when they have to add 3/4 and 2/3 in their head. The good news is that each of the four fraction operations follows a simple, repeatable rule. Learn the rule once, and any fraction problem becomes mechanical.\n     2→\n     3→This guide walks through **addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division of fractions** with worked examples for each, plus a section on mixed numbers. Skip to the operation you need, or use the [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) to check your work instantly.\n     4→\n     5→## The Four Rules at a Glance\n     6→\n     7→| Operation | Rule | Example |\n     8→|---|---|---|\n     9→| Addition (a/b + c/d) | Find a common denominator, add numerators | 1/2 + 1/3 = 3/6 + 2/6 = **5/6** |\n    10→| Subtraction (a/b − c/d) | Find a common denominator, subtract numerators | 3/4 − 1/6 = 9/12 − 2/12 = **7/12** |\n    11→| Multiplication (a/b × c/d) | Multiply across: (a×c)/(b×d) | 2/3 × 3/4 = 6/12 = **1/2** |\n    12→| Division (a/b ÷ c/d) | Multiply by reciprocal: (a/b) × (d/c) | 2/3 ÷ 4/5 = 2/3 × 5/4 = **5/6** |\n    13→\n    14→## How to Add Fractions\n    15→\n    16→Adding fractions requires a **common denominator** — both fractions must share the same bottom number before you can add the tops.\n    17→\n    18→**Step 1: Check the denominators.** If they're the same, skip to step 3. If not, find a common denominator.\n    19→\n    20→**Step 2: Find the common denominator.** The easiest method: multiply the two denominators together. You can also find the least common multiple (LCM) for cleaner numbers, but multiplying always works.\n    21→\n    22→**Step 3: Convert each fraction.** Multiply the numerator and denominator of each fraction so both denominators become your common denominator.\n    23→\n    24→**Step 4: Add the numerators.** Keep the common denominator.\n    25→\n    26→**Step 5: Simplify.** Divide numerator and denominator by their Greatest Common Factor (GCF).\n    27→\n    28→### Example: 1/2 + 1/3\n    29→\n    30→1. Different denominators: 2 and 3\n    31→2. Common denominator: 2 × 3 = **6**\n    32→3. Convert: 1/2 → 3/6 and 1/3 → 2/6\n    33→4. Add: 3/6 + 2/6 = **5/6**\n    34→5. Already simplified (GCF of 5 and 6 is 1)\n    35→\n    36→### Example: 3/8 + 1/4\n    37→\n    38→1. Common denominator: 8 (since 4 goes into 8 evenly)\n    39→2. Convert: 1/4 → 2/8\n    40→3. Add: 3/8 + 2/8 = **5/8** ✓\n    41→\n    42→<div class=\"callout\">\n    43→<strong>Same-denominator shortcut:</strong> When denominators already match, just add the numerators. 3/7 + 2/7 = 5/7. Done.\n    44→</div>\n    45→\n    46→## How to Subtract Fractions\n    47→\n    48→Subtraction follows the exact same steps as addition — the only difference is that you subtract the numerators in step 4.\n    49→\n    50→### Example: 5/6 − 1/4\n    51→\n    52→1. Different denominators: 6 and 4\n    53→2. Common denominator: 6 × 4 = 24 (or LCM = 12, which keeps numbers smaller)\n    54→3. Using 12: 5/6 → 10/12, 1/4 → 3/12\n    55→4. Subtract: 10/12 − 3/12 = **7/12**\n    56→5. GCF(7, 12) = 1, already simplified ✓\n    57→\n    58→### Example: 7/10 − 3/10\n    59→\n    60→Same denominator: 7/10 − 3/10 = **4/10 = 2/5** (simplified by dividing by 2)\n    61→\n    62→Use the [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) to verify any subtraction and see every step shown automatically.\n    63→\n    64→## How to Multiply Fractions\n    65→\n    66→Multiplication is the easiest operation: **no common denominator needed**. Multiply straight across.\n    67→\n    68→**Rule:** (a/b) × (c/d) = (a×c) / (b×d), then simplify.\n    69→\n    70→### Example: 2/3 × 3/4\n    71→\n    72→- Multiply numerators: 2 × 3 = 6\n    73→- Multiply denominators: 3 × 4 = 12\n    74→- Result: 6/12 = **1/2** (simplified by GCF = 6)\n    75→\n    76→### Example: 5/6 × 4/9\n    77→\n    78→- 5 × 4 = 20, 6 × 9 = 54\n    79→- 20/54 → GCF(20, 54) = 2 → **10/27**\n    80→\n    81→**Cross-cancellation tip:** Before multiplying, check if any numerator and diagonal denominator share a common factor. Cancel them first to keep numbers small. For 2/3 × 3/4: the 3 in the numerator of the second fraction and the 3 in the denominator of the first cancel to 1, giving 2/1 × 1/4 = 2/4 = 1/2. Same answer, smaller arithmetic.\n    82→\n    83→## How to Divide Fractions\n    84→\n    85→Division uses the \"keep, change, flip\" method:\n    86→\n    87→1. **Keep** the first fraction as-is\n    88→2. **Change** ÷ to ×\n    89→3. **Flip** the second fraction (take its reciprocal)\n    90→4. Multiply normally and simplify\n    91→\n    92→### Example: 3/4 ÷ 1/2\n    93→\n    94→- Keep 3/4, change to ×, flip 1/2 to 2/1\n    95→- 3/4 × 2/1 = 6/4 = **3/2 = 1 1/2**\n    96→\n    97→### Example: 5/8 ÷ 5/4\n    98→\n    99→- Flip 5/4 → 4/5\n   100→- 5/8 × 4/5 = 20/40 = **1/2** ✓\n   101→\n   102→<div class=\"callout\">\n   103→<strong>Why does \"flip and multiply\" work?</strong> Dividing by a number is the same as multiplying by its reciprocal. 6 ÷ 2 = 6 × (1/2) = 3. The same logic extends to fractions.\n   104→</div>\n   105→\n   106→## Working with Mixed Numbers\n   107→\n   108→A mixed number combines a whole number and a fraction (like 2 3/4). Before you can do arithmetic, convert it to an improper fraction:\n   109→\n   110→**Formula:** Whole × Denominator + Numerator, over the original denominator\n   111→\n   112→| Mixed Number | Conversion | Improper Fraction |\n   113→|---|---|---|\n   114→| 1 1/2 | (1×2)+1 / 2 | 3/2 |\n   115→| 2 3/4 | (2×4)+3 / 4 | 11/4 |\n   116→| 3 2/3 | (3×3)+2 / 3 | 11/3 |\n   117→| 4 1/8 | (4×8)+1 / 8 | 33/8 |\n   118→\n   119→### Example: 1 1/2 + 2/3\n   120→\n   121→1. Convert 1 1/2 → 3/2\n   122→2. Add: 3/2 + 2/3 — find common denominator 6\n   123→3. 9/6 + 4/6 = 13/6\n   124→4. Convert back to mixed number: 13 ÷ 6 = 2 with remainder 1, so **2 1/6**\n   125→\n   126→### Example: 2 3/4 × 1 1/3\n   127→\n   128→1. Convert: 2 3/4 → 11/4 and 1 1/3 → 4/3\n   129→2. Multiply: 11/4 × 4/3 = 44/12 = **11/3 = 3 2/3**\n   130→\n   131→The [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) handles mixed number input directly — type the whole number into the \"Whole Number\" field and let the calculator do the conversion.\n   132→\n   133→## Simplifying Fractions (Reducing to Lowest Terms)\n   134→\n   135→A fraction is **fully simplified** when the numerator and denominator share no common factor other than 1.\n   136→\n   137→**Method:** Find the GCF of the numerator and denominator, then divide both by it.\n   138→\n   139→| Fraction | GCF | Simplified |\n   140→|---|---|---|\n   141→| 6/12 | 6 | 1/2 |\n   142→| 15/25 | 5 | 3/5 |\n   143→| 18/24 | 6 | 3/4 |\n   144→| 14/21 | 7 | 2/3 |\n   145→\n   146→**Finding the GCF:** List the factors of each number and find the largest one they share, or use the Euclidean algorithm (divide the larger by the smaller, take the remainder, repeat until remainder is 0).\n   147→\n   148→Fractions also relate directly to percentages — for quick conversions between the two, the [percentage calculator](/calculators/percentage-calculator) is useful. When working out discounts (e.g., a 1/4-off sale = 25% off), the [discount calculator](/calculators/discount-calculator) gives you the final price instantly.\n   149→\n   150→## Frequently Asked Questions\n   151→\n   152→**What is a common denominator and why do I need one to add or subtract fractions?**\n   153→A common denominator is a shared bottom number that lets you compare or combine fractions. You can only add or subtract fractions when they represent equal-sized parts. 1/4 and 1/3 are different-sized pieces, so you can't directly add them — you first convert both to twelfths (3/12 and 4/12) and then add to get 7/12.\n   154→\n   155→**Can the denominator be negative?**\n   156→By convention, fractions are written with a positive denominator. If you end up with a negative denominator, multiply both numerator and denominator by −1. For example, 3/(−4) = (−3)/4.\n   157→\n   158→**What is an improper fraction?**\n   159→An improper fraction has a numerator larger than (or equal to) its denominator, such as 7/4. It represents a value greater than 1. It can also be written as the mixed number 1 3/4. Both forms are equally correct; improper fractions are easier to multiply and divide.\n   160→\n   161→**How do I add three or more fractions?**\n   162→Add two fractions at a time. Find a common denominator for the first two, add them, then add the result to the third fraction. Or find a common denominator for all fractions at once (the LCM of all denominators) and convert each.\n   163→\n   164→**Do I always need to simplify my answer?**\n   165→In most school and everyday contexts, yes — a simplified fraction is the expected final form. The [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) automatically simplifies every result for you.\n   166→\n   167→## The Bottom Line\n   168→\n   169→Every fraction operation follows a simple pattern:\n   170→\n   171→- **Add/Subtract:** common denominator first, then combine numerators\n   172→- **Multiply:** straight across, then simplify\n   173→- **Divide:** flip the second fraction, then multiply\n   174→\n   175→For mixed numbers, convert to improper fractions before operating. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_018dJ7VuQC5hdna2Pyqdfr5b","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01NDnodYGTwD23WSHSL5riU1","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/home/runner/work/calccrunch/calccrunch/src/content/blog/how-to-calculate-percentage-change.md","content":"Percentage change measures how much a value has grown or shrunk relative to its starting point. Whether you're tracking a stock price, comparing last month's electricity bill, or figuring out how much a salary raise is really worth, the formula is always the same — and it takes about ten seconds once you know it.\n\n**The short answer:** Percentage change = ((New Value − Old Value) / |Old Value|) × 100\n\nA positive result is an increase; a negative result is a decrease.\n\n## The Percentage Change Formula\n\n$$\\text{Percentage Change} = \\frac{\\text{New Value} - \\text{Old Value}}{|\\text{Old Value}|} \\times 100$$\n\nThe absolute value bars around the old value ensure the formula works correctly even when starting from a negative number (see the FAQ section). For most everyday use with positive values, you can ignore the bars.\n\nUse the [percentage calculator](/calculators/percentage-calculator) to run any of these calculations instantly.\n\n## Step-by-Step Examples\n\n### Example 1: Price Increase\n\nA jacket costs $80 in January and $96 in March. What is the percentage increase?\n\n1. New − Old = $96 − $80 = **$16**\n2. Divide by old: $16 / $80 = **0.20**\n3. Multiply by 100: 0.20 × 100 = **+20%**\n\nThe jacket's price increased by 20%.\n\n### Example 2: Price Decrease (Sale)\n\nA laptop was $1,200 and is now on sale for $900. What is the percentage decrease?\n\n1. New − Old = $900 − $1,200 = **−$300**\n2. Divide by old: −$300 / $1,200 = **−0.25**\n3. Multiply by 100: −0.25 × 100 = **−25%**\n\nThe laptop is 25% off. For discount math, the [discount calculator](/calculators/discount-calculator) also calculates the dollar savings and final price in one step.\n\n### Example 3: Salary Raise\n\nYou earn $52,000 and receive a raise to $56,160. What percentage raise is that?\n\n1. $56,160 − $52,000 = **$4,160**\n2. $4,160 / $52,000 = **0.08**\n3. 0.08 × 100 = **+8%**\n\nAn 8% raise.\n\n### Example 4: Test Score Change\n\nYou scored 68 on your first quiz and 85 on your second. What's the percentage improvement?\n\n1. 85 − 68 = **17**\n2. 17 / 68 = **0.25**\n3. 0.25 × 100 = **+25%**\n\nA 25% improvement in score.\n\n## Common Percentage Change Scenarios\n\n| Scenario | Old Value | New Value | Change |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| Grocery price increase | $3.50 | $4.20 | +20% |\n| Gas price drop | $4.00 | $3.40 | −15% |\n| Portfolio growth (1 year) | $10,000 | $11,350 | +13.5% |\n| Monthly rent increase | $1,500 | $1,575 | +5% |\n| Sales decline quarter-over-quarter | 240 units | 204 units | −15% |\n\n## Percentage Increase vs. Percentage Decrease\n\nThe same formula covers both directions — the sign of the result tells you which:\n\n- **Positive result → increase** (new > old)\n- **Negative result → decrease** (new < old)\n- **Zero → no change**\n\nMany people confuse percentage change with percentage points. If an interest rate goes from 2% to 3%, that is a change of **1 percentage point** (simple subtraction), but it is a **50% increase** in the rate (using the percentage change formula). These are different things.\n\n## The Reverse Calculation: Finding the Original Value\n\nSometimes you know the final value and the percentage change, and you need to find the starting value.\n\n**Formula:** Original = New Value / (1 + (Percentage Change / 100))\n\n**Example:** A product now costs $126 after a 5% price increase. What was the original price?\n\n- Original = $126 / (1 + 0.05) = $126 / 1.05 = **$120**\n\n**Example:** A stock is down 20% from its peak and now trades at $64. What was the peak price?\n\n- Original = $64 / (1 − 0.20) = $64 / 0.80 = **$80**\n\n## The Symmetry Trap: Why +X% Then −X% ≠ 0%\n\nA common misconception: if something increases by 25% and then decreases by 25%, you'd expect to be back where you started. You're not.\n\nStarting with $100:\n- +25%: $100 × 1.25 = **$125**\n- −25% of $125: $125 × 0.75 = **$93.75**\n\nYou end up at $93.75, not $100. The loss of 25% applies to a larger base ($125), so it removes more in absolute terms than the original gain. This asymmetry is important to understand for investments, pricing, and any repeated percent changes.\n\n## Percentage Change vs. Percentage Difference\n\nThese two terms are often mixed up:\n\n- **Percentage change** compares a new value to an old value. Direction matters: the \"old\" is the reference point.\n- **Percentage difference** is used when there is no clear \"before\" and \"after\" — for example, comparing the prices of the same item at two different stores. The formula uses the average of the two values as the denominator: |V1 − V2| / ((V1 + V2)/2) × 100.\n\nFractions and percentages are closely linked — a 1/4 price reduction is the same as a 25% decrease, a 3/8 discount equals 37.5% off. The [fraction calculator](/calculators/fraction-calculator) can help convert fraction-based discounts to their decimal form before plugging into the percentage change formula.\n\n## Frequently Asked Questions\n\n**What is the percentage change formula?**\n((New Value − Old Value) / |Old Value|) × 100. A positive result means an increase; a negative result means a decrease.\n\n**What if the old value is zero?**\nPercentage change is undefined when the old value is zero, because you cannot divide by zero. If you're comparing to a starting value of zero (like first-month revenue), describe the change in absolute terms instead (e.g., \"grew from $0 to $5,000\") or use a different metric.\n\n**Can percentage change be more than 100%?**\nYes. If a value doubles, that is a 100% increase. If it triples, that's 200%. There is no upper ceiling on percentage increase.\n\n**How do I calculate percentage change in Excel or Google Sheets?**\nUse the formula =(B2-A2)/ABS(A2)*100, where A2 is the old value and B2 is the new value. Format the cell as a number or percentage.\n\n**Is a 50% increase followed by a 50% decrease the same as no change?**\nNo. Start at 100 → +50% → 150 → −50% → 75. You lose 25% overall. This is the symmetry trap described above.\n\n**How is percentage change different from percentage points?**\nPercentage change uses the original value as the base (relative). Percentage points are an absolute difference between two percentages. If inflation rises from 3% to 5%, that's 2 percentage points but a 66.7% percentage change in the inflation rate.\n\n## The Bottom Line\n\nThe percentage change formula is straightforward: subtract the old value from the new, divide by the old value, and multiply by 100. A positive number means growth; a negative number means decline.\n\nKeep two things in mind:\n1. The old (original) value is always the denominator — direction matters.\n2. Percentage change and percentage points are different things.\n\nFor quick calculations, the [percentage calculator](/calculators/percentage-calculator) handles any percentage problem, including change, in seconds. For discount-based scenarios, the [discount calculator](/calculators/discount-calculator) goes further and shows your total savings and final price in one step.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"stop_details":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":288,"cache_read_input_tokens":82235,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":288,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":65,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"}},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"0aba2aef-5370-4f6d-992f-47ee58dd3d79","uuid":"d1f5f49c-a7be-49ad-80e7-ef42ad4e1a3d"}
